type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@id":"https://www.scmp.com/week-asia/politics/article/3263766/south-china-sea-philippines-warns-china-against-arrest-its-fishermen-under-beijings-trespassing-law","@type":"WebPage"}</script><script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":["NewsArticle","ReportageNewsArticle"],"alternativeHeadline":"‘Unacceptable’: Manila warns Beijing against arrest under ‘trespassing’ law","articleBody":"The Philippines has vowed not to allow China’s coastguard to arrest Filipino fishermen accused of trespassing in its maritime territory, warning it will lodge a diplomatic protest should Beijing proceed with its controversial new policy.\nPhilippine Navy spokesman Commodore Roy Trinidad told reporters on Wednesday that China’s move – set to take effect on June 15 – was “unacceptable” and Manila had contingency plans to counter its actions.\n“Not only the Philippine Navy but the entire government and nation will not allow this arrest, based on the pronouncement of our commander-in-chief that this is totally unacceptable,” Trinidad warned, referring to a directive by President Ferdinand Marcos Jnr. He did not elaborate on what the contingency plans might be.\nThe latest war of words between both countries, which have been locked in a months-long territorial row in the South China Sea, centres on a new regulation under which the Chinese coastguard can detain foreign nationals for up to 60 days if they are caught within the maritime territory claimed by Beijing. Such a move lacked any basis in international law, Trinidad said.\nOn Monday , the Philippines called on China to let international investigators examine Scarborough Shoal after it accused Beijing of damaging coral reefs in the area as part of reclamation plans.\nIn 2012, China seized control of the Scarborough Shoal, a traditional fishing ground within the 200-nautical-mile exclusive economic zone of the Philippines, after a two-month stand-off with the Philippine Navy.\n\nFollowing a meeting of the Senate Committee on National Defence and Security, Peace, Unification and Reconciliation on Wednesday, a senior official from the Department of Justice said Manila could file a diplomatic protest against China’s latest coastguard policy.\n“Once we confirm that the provisions are indeed a violation of international law including the arbitral award because, for example, that is our exclusive economic zone, they should not arrest our fishermen when they go to Scarborough Shoal. That is their traditional fishing ground … And so we can file a diplomatic protest,” said the department’s Senior State Counsel Fretti Ganchoon.\nAccording to Ganchoon, China could face legal repercussions if it enacts regulations that violate international law or impact other nations.\nChina claims most of the mineral-rich South China Sea. The region is also claimed by Brunei, Malaysia, the Philippines and Vietnam, and there have been numerous territorial clashes in the area, particularly between Chinese and Philippine vessels.\nIn recent months, China’s actions in the West Philippine Sea have become increasingly aggressive. The West Philippine Sea is Manila’s term for the section of the South China Sea that defines its maritime territory and includes its exclusive economic zone.\n\nOn April 30, the Chinese coastguard fired a water cannon at a Philippine vessel that was distributing fuel and food supplies to Filipino fishermen near Scarborough Shoal.\nOn March 23, Manila’s regular resupply mission to its outpost on Second Thomas Shoal was disrupted by two Chinese coastguard vessels that fired water cannons at a civilian resupply boat, leaving three sailors injured.\nA Chinese coastguard vessel also shattered the windshield of a Philippine resupply boat, the Unaizah May 4, injuring four Filipino sailors while they were on a resupply mission on March 5.\nForeign Secretary Enrique Manalo said at a press briefing on Wednesday that Manila had always sought to manage the dispute in the West Philippine Sea diplomatically but that China was “not helping Manila to do so”.\n\n\nWhen asked about the current bilateral ties given the incidents in the South China Sea, Manalo said: “I would say they’re a bit choppy. These actions have been a cause for raising tensions. We’re merely trying to assert our rights. Unfortunately, we’re being hampered in doing so and in my personal belief, this is creating tensions.”\nWhen asked if tensions would worsen, Manalo added: “I don’t think we’re there yet. We have to find a way to manage. But we have to have an understanding on how we can manage our relationship without increasing tensions.\n“China is not really helping us. That’s the challenge because of the many incidents that are occurring. But we’re still committed to seeking diplomatic means to manage our tensions.”\nFormer Senate President Juan Miguel Zubiri said in a radio interview on Sunday that China would only worsen the situation in the region if it were to apprehend “trespassers” in the South China Sea.\n“I am appealing to the Chinese government not to force that on us because it would only escalate the tension.”\n","articleSection":"Politics","author":{"@type":"Person","name":"Jeoffrey data-qa="ArticleSpeechInlineWidgetGeneric-Wrapper" class="css-fma335 egghhyv1"><div class="e1oct4f93 css-a5jxnj e9t53i10" data-qa="EmptyButton-Container"><div data-qa="EmptyButton-IconWrapper" class="css-1duflnb e9t53i12"><svg width="28" height="24" viewBox="0 0 28 24" fill="none" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" data-qa="EmptyButton-StyledIcon" class="css-ozd7xs e9t53i11"><path d="M14 eg5341w15"><section data-qa="ContentBody-ContentBodyContainer" class="e1oct4f97 css-19sk4h4 e1n4d2ln0"><div datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1xdhyk6 e1346ty30"><a class="e13op9j60 e1qbu6wp1 css-1ankfgb ef1hf1w0" href="https://www.scmp.com/topics/philippines?module=inline&amp;pgtype=article" data-qa="BaseLink-renderAnchor-StyledAnchor"><span data-qa="Component-Text" class="css-0 e1qbu6wp0">The Philippines</span></a> has vowed not to allow <a class="e13op9j60 e1qbu6wp1 css-1ankfgb ef1hf1w0" href="https://www.scmp.com/news/china?module=inline&amp;pgtype=article" data-qa="BaseLink-renderAnchor-StyledAnchor"><span data-qa="Component-Text" class="css-0 e1qbu6wp0">China</span></a>’s coastguard to arrest Filipino fishermen accused of <a class="e13op9j60 e1qbu6wp1 css-1ankfgb ef1hf1w0" href="https://www.scmp.com/week-asia/politics/article/3263323/south-china-sea-philippines-may-be-forced-sue-if-chinese-coastguard-arrests-trespassers?module=inline&amp;pgtype=article" data-qa="BaseLink-renderAnchor-StyledAnchor"><span data-qa="Component-Text" class="css-0 e1qbu6wp0">trespassing</span></a> in its maritime territory, warning it will lodge a diplomatic protest should Beijing proceed with its controversial new policy.</div><div class="js-reading-0-percent-completion-tracker css-19icq8a e1gsc5vp0" data-qa="useTrackViewedPercentageRender-trackViewedPercentageRenders-TrackPercentageBlock"></div><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">Philippine Navy spokesman Commodore Roy Trinidad told reporters on Wednesday that China’s move – set to take effect on June 15 – was “unacceptable” and Manila had contingency plans to counter its actions.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">“Not only the Philippine Navy but the entire government and nation will not allow this arrest, based on the pronouncement of our commander-in-chief that this is totally unacceptable,” Trinidad warned, referring to a directive by President Ferdinand Marcos Jnr. He did not elaborate on what the contingency plans might be.</p><div datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1xdhyk6 e1346ty30">The latest war of words between both countries, which have been locked in a months-long territorial row in the <a class="e13op9j60 e1qbu6wp1 css-1ankfgb ef1hf1w0" href="https://www.scmp.com/topics/south-china-sea?module=inline&amp;pgtype=article" data-qa="BaseLink-renderAnchor-StyledAnchor"><span data-qa="Component-Text" class="css-0 e1qbu6wp0">South China Sea</span></a>, centres on a new regulation under which the Chinese coastguard can detain foreign nationals for up to 60 days if they are caught within the maritime territory claimed by Beijing. Such a move lacked any basis in international law, Trinidad said.</div></section><section data-qa="ContentBody-ContentBodyContainer" class=" e1oct4f96 css-1myfomw e1n4d2ln0"><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">On Monday , the Philippines called on China to let international investigators examine Scarborough Shoal after it accused Beijing of damaging coral reefs in the area as part of reclamation plans.</p><div class="js-reading-20-percent-completion-tracker css-19icq8a e1gsc5vp0" data-qa="useTrackViewedPercentageRender-trackViewedPercentageRenders-TrackPercentageBlock"></div><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">In 2012, China seized control of the Scarborough Shoal, a traditional fishing ground within the 200-nautical-mile exclusive economic zone of the Philippines, after a two-month stand-off with the Philippine Navy.</p><div class="image-inline-container e1kkz63z0 css-1llrc1m ecv2zmr0" data-qa="Component-renderMap-StyledDiv"><div data-qa="Component-Container" class="css-bjn8wh e1xb2kle0"><figure class="image-inline caption e76tpiq0 e1xb2kle1 css-18ms6eb eope0cl0" data-qa="ArticleImage-ArticleImageContainer"><div data-qa="ArticleImage-ImageContainer" class="css-0 eope0cl3"><img data-qa="BaseImage-handleRenderImage-StyledImage" class="eope0cl2 css-3q8izr em4672x0" loading="lazy" src="https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/d8/images/canvas/2024/05/23/af7a4ba0-2c0a-4279-ba51-12e6a0945eaf_e3f5a864.jpg" title="The Philippines has blamed Chinese fishermen for the massive loss of giant clams in the disputed Scarborough Shoal guarded by Beijing’s coastguard in the South China Sea. Photo: AP"/><script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"ImageObject","caption":"The Philippines has blamed Chinese fishermen for the massive loss of giant clams in the disputed Scarborough Shoal guarded by Beijing’s coastguard in the South China Sea. Photo: AP","url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/d8/images/canvas/2024/05/23/af7a4ba0-2c0a-4279-ba51-12e6a0945eaf_e3f5a864.jpg"}</script></div><figcaption data-qa="ArticleImage-DescriptionContainer" class="css-6potyr eope0cl1">The Philippines has blamed Chinese fishermen for the massive loss of giant clams in the disputed Scarborough Shoal guarded by Beijing’s coastguard in the South China Sea. Photo: AP</figcaption></figure></div></div><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">Following a meeting of the Senate Committee on National Defence and Security, Peace, Unification and Reconciliation on Wednesday, a senior official from the Department of Justice said Manila could file a diplomatic protest against China’s latest coastguard policy.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">“Once we confirm that the provisions are indeed a violation of international law including the arbitral award because, for example, that is our exclusive economic zone, they should not arrest our fishermen when they go to Scarborough Shoal. That is their traditional fishing ground … And so we can file a diplomatic protest,” said the department’s Senior State Counsel Fretti Ganchoon.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">According to Ganchoon, China could face legal repercussions if it enacts regulations that violate international law or impact other nations.</p><div data-qa="Component-Container" class=" css-1jyke50 e1cwk53e0"></div><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">China claims most of the mineral-rich South China Sea. The region is also claimed by Brunei, Malaysia, the Philippines and Vietnam, and there have been numerous territorial clashes in the area, particularly between Chinese and Philippine vessels.</p><div class="js-reading-50-percent-completion-tracker css-19icq8a e1gsc5vp0" data-qa="useTrackViewedPercentageRender-trackViewedPercentageRenders-TrackPercentageBlock"></div><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">In recent months, China’s actions in the West Philippine Sea have become increasingly aggressive. The West Philippine Sea is Manila’s term for the section of the South China Sea that defines its maritime territory and includes its exclusive economic zone.</p><div class="image-inline-container e1kkz63z0 css-1llrc1m ecv2zmr0" data-qa="Component-renderMap-StyledDiv"><div data-qa="Component-Container" class="css-bjn8wh e1xb2kle0"><figure class="image-inline caption e76tpiq0 e1xb2kle1 css-18ms6eb eope0cl0" data-qa="ArticleImage-ArticleImageContainer"><div data-qa="ArticleImage-ImageContainer" class="css-0 eope0cl3"><img data-qa="BaseImage-handleRenderImage-StyledImage" class="eope0cl2 css-3q8izr em4672x0" loading="lazy" src="https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/d8/images/canvas/2024/05/23/aba685bf-dab0-4834-bb1d-467ed6b3ab9e_595de0ff.jpg" title="Chinese coastguard vessels fire water cannons at a Philippine resupply vessel Unaizah on May 4 as it sailed towards the Second Thomas Shoal in the South China Sea in March. Photo: Reuters"/><script type="application/ld+json">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"ImageObject","caption":"Chinese coastguard vessels fire water cannons at a Philippine resupply vessel Unaizah on May 4 as it sailed towards the Second Thomas Shoal in the South China Sea in March. Photo: Reuters","url":"https://cdn.i-scmp.com/sites/default/files/d8/images/canvas/2024/05/23/aba685bf-dab0-4834-bb1d-467ed6b3ab9e_595de0ff.jpg"}</script></div><figcaption data-qa="ArticleImage-DescriptionContainer" class="css-6potyr eope0cl1">Chinese coastguard vessels fire water cannons at a Philippine resupply vessel Unaizah on May 4 as it sailed towards the Second Thomas Shoal in the South China Sea in March. Photo: Reuters</figcaption></figure></div></div><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">On April 30, the Chinese coastguard fired a water cannon at a Philippine vessel that was distributing fuel and food supplies to Filipino fishermen near Scarborough Shoal.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">On March 23, Manila’s regular resupply mission to its outpost on Second Thomas Shoal was disrupted by two Chinese coastguard vessels that fired water cannons at a civilian resupply boat, leaving three sailors injured.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">A Chinese coastguard vessel also shattered the windshield of a Philippine resupply boat, the Unaizah May 4, injuring four Filipino sailors while they were on a resupply mission on March 5.</p><p datatype="p" data-qa="Component-Component" class="e15kmbpe0 css-1c6uqr6 e1346ty31">Foreign Secretary Enrique Manalo said at a press briefing on Wednesday that Manila had always sought to manage the dispute in the West Philippine Sea diplomatically but that China was “not helping Manila to do so”.</p><div class="methode-html-wrapper oembed-wrapper e1kkz63z0 css-1llrc1m ecv2zmr0" data-qa="Component-renderMap-StyledDiv">

Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing.\n","json":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","data":"The Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing."}]}]},"subHeadline":{"text":"The Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing\nForeign Secretary Enrique Manalo says Manila is committed to easing tensions in the South China Sea despite Beijing’s new law\n","json":[{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"The Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"Foreign Secretary Enrique Manalo says Manila is committed to easing tensions in the South China Sea despite Beijing’s new law"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"}]}]},"body":{"text":"The Philippines has vowed not to allow China’s coastguard to arrest Filipino fishermen accused of trespassing in its maritime territory, warning it will lodge a diplomatic protest should Beijing proceed with its controversial new policy.\nPhilippine Navy spokesman Commodore Roy Trinidad told reporters on Wednesday that China’s move – set to take effect on June 15 – was “unacceptable” and Manila had contingency plans to counter its actions.\n“Not only the Philippine Navy but the entire government and nation will not allow this arrest, based on the pronouncement of our commander-in-chief that this is totally unacceptable,” Trinidad warned, referring to a directive by President Ferdinand Marcos Jnr. He did not elaborate on what the contingency plans might be.\nThe latest war of words between both countries, which have been locked in a months-long territorial row in the South China Sea, centres on a new regulation under which the Chinese coastguard can detain foreign nationals for up to 60 days if they are caught within the maritime territory claimed by Beijing. Such a move lacked any basis in international law, Trinidad said.\nOn Monday , the Philippines called on China to let international investigators examine Scarborough Shoal after it accused Beijing of damaging coral reefs in the area as part of reclamation plans.\nIn 2012, China seized control of the Scarborough Shoal, a traditional fishing ground within the 200-nautical-mile exclusive economic zone of the Philippines, after a two-month stand-off with the Philippine Navy.\n\nFollowing a meeting of the Senate Committee on National Defence and Security, Peace, Unification and Reconciliation on Wednesday, a senior official from the Department of Justice said Manila could file a diplomatic protest against China’s latest coastguard policy.\n“Once we confirm that the provisions are indeed a violation of international law including the arbitral award because, for example, that is our exclusive economic zone, they should not arrest our fishermen when they go to Scarborough Shoal. That is their traditional fishing ground … And so we can file a diplomatic protest,” said the department’s Senior State Counsel Fretti Ganchoon.\nAccording to Ganchoon, China could face legal repercussions if it enacts regulations that violate international law or impact other nations.\nChina claims most of the mineral-rich South China Sea. The region is also claimed by Brunei, Malaysia, the Philippines and Vietnam, and there have been numerous territorial clashes in the area, particularly between Chinese and Philippine vessels.\nIn recent months, China’s actions in the West Philippine Sea have become increasingly aggressive. The West Philippine Sea is Manila’s term for the section of the South China Sea that defines its maritime territory and includes its exclusive economic zone.\n\nOn April 30, the Chinese coastguard fired a water cannon at a Philippine vessel that was distributing fuel and food supplies to Filipino fishermen near Scarborough Shoal.\nOn March 23, Manila’s regular resupply mission to its outpost on Second Thomas Shoal was disrupted by two Chinese coastguard vessels that fired water cannons at a civilian resupply boat, leaving three sailors injured.\nA Chinese coastguard vessel also shattered the windshield of a Philippine resupply boat, the Unaizah May 4, injuring four Filipino sailors while they were on a resupply mission on March 5.\nForeign Secretary Enrique Manalo said at a press briefing on Wednesday that Manila had always sought to manage the dispute in the West Philippine Sea diplomatically but that China was “not helping Manila to do so”.\n\n\nWhen asked about the current bilateral ties given the incidents in the South China Sea, Manalo said: “I would say they’re a bit choppy. These actions have been a cause for raising tensions. We’re merely trying to assert our rights. Unfortunately, we’re being hampered in doing so and in my personal belief, this is creating tensions.”\nWhen asked if tensions would worsen, Manalo added: “I don’t think we’re there yet. We have to find a way to manage. But we have to have an understanding on how we can manage our relationship without increasing tensions.\n“China is not really helping us. That’s the challenge because of the many incidents that are occurring. But we’re still committed to seeking diplomatic means to manage our tensions.”\nFormer Senate President Juan Miguel Zubiri said in a radio interview on Sunday that China would only worsen the situation in the region if it were to apprehend “trespassers” in the South China Sea.\n“I am appealing to the Chinese government not to force that on us because it would only escalate the tension.”\n","json":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"a","attribs":{"href":"https://www.scmp.com/topics/philippines","title":"","target":"_self","data-entity-uuid":"3a83a0e4-5306-449f-82e7-7e069dc9e7f3","data-entity-type":"taxonomy_term","data-entity-bundle":"topics"},"children":[{"type":"text","data":"The Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing.\n","json":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"text","data":"The Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing."}]}]},"subHeadline":{"text":"The Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing\nForeign Secretary Enrique Manalo says Manila is committed to easing tensions in the South China Sea despite Beijing’s new law\n","json":[{"type":"ul","children":[{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"The Philippines says it has contingency plans to counter China’s actions including filing a diplomatic protest against Beijing"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"},{"type":"li","children":[{"type":"text","data":"Foreign Secretary Enrique Manalo says Manila is committed to easing tensions in the South China Sea despite Beijing’s new law"}]},{"type":"text","data":"\n"}]}]},"body":{"text":"The Philippines has vowed not to allow China’s coastguard to arrest Filipino fishermen accused of trespassing in its maritime territory, warning it will lodge a diplomatic protest should Beijing proceed with its controversial new policy.\nPhilippine Navy spokesman Commodore Roy Trinidad told reporters on Wednesday that China’s move – set to take effect on June 15 – was “unacceptable” and Manila had contingency plans to counter its actions.\n“Not only the Philippine Navy but the entire government and nation will not allow this arrest, based on the pronouncement of our commander-in-chief that this is totally unacceptable,” Trinidad warned, referring to a directive by President Ferdinand Marcos Jnr. He did not elaborate on what the contingency plans might be.\nThe latest war of words between both countries, which have been locked in a months-long territorial row in the South China Sea, centres on a new regulation under which the Chinese coastguard can detain foreign nationals for up to 60 days if they are caught within the maritime territory claimed by Beijing. Such a move lacked any basis in international law, Trinidad said.\nOn Monday , the Philippines called on China to let international investigators examine Scarborough Shoal after it accused Beijing of damaging coral reefs in the area as part of reclamation plans.\nIn 2012, China seized control of the Scarborough Shoal, a traditional fishing ground within the 200-nautical-mile exclusive economic zone of the Philippines, after a two-month stand-off with the Philippine Navy.\n\nFollowing a meeting of the Senate Committee on National Defence and Security, Peace, Unification and Reconciliation on Wednesday, a senior official from the Department of Justice said Manila could file a diplomatic protest against China’s latest coastguard policy.\n“Once we confirm that the provisions are indeed a violation of international law including the arbitral award because, for example, that is our exclusive economic zone, they should not arrest our fishermen when they go to Scarborough Shoal. That is their traditional fishing ground … And so we can file a diplomatic protest,” said the department’s Senior State Counsel Fretti Ganchoon.\nAccording to Ganchoon, China could face legal repercussions if it enacts regulations that violate international law or impact other nations.\nChina claims most of the mineral-rich South China Sea. The region is also claimed by Brunei, Malaysia, the Philippines and Vietnam, and there have been numerous territorial clashes in the area, particularly between Chinese and Philippine vessels.\nIn recent months, China’s actions in the West Philippine Sea have become increasingly aggressive. The West Philippine Sea is Manila’s term for the section of the South China Sea that defines its maritime territory and includes its exclusive economic zone.\n\nOn April 30, the Chinese coastguard fired a water cannon at a Philippine vessel that was distributing fuel and food supplies to Filipino fishermen near Scarborough Shoal.\nOn March 23, Manila’s regular resupply mission to its outpost on Second Thomas Shoal was disrupted by two Chinese coastguard vessels that fired water cannons at a civilian resupply boat, leaving three sailors injured.\nA Chinese coastguard vessel also shattered the windshield of a Philippine resupply boat, the Unaizah May 4, injuring four Filipino sailors while they were on a resupply mission on March 5.\nForeign Secretary Enrique Manalo said at a press briefing on Wednesday that Manila had always sought to manage the dispute in the West Philippine Sea diplomatically but that China was “not helping Manila to do so”.\n\n\nWhen asked about the current bilateral ties given the incidents in the South China Sea, Manalo said: “I would say they’re a bit choppy. These actions have been a cause for raising tensions. We’re merely trying to assert our rights. Unfortunately, we’re being hampered in doing so and in my personal belief, this is creating tensions.”\nWhen asked if tensions would worsen, Manalo added: “I don’t think we’re there yet. We have to find a way to manage. But we have to have an understanding on how we can manage our relationship without increasing tensions.\n“China is not really helping us. That’s the challenge because of the many incidents that are occurring. But we’re still committed to seeking diplomatic means to manage our tensions.”\nFormer Senate President Juan Miguel Zubiri said in a radio interview on Sunday that China would only worsen the situation in the region if it were to apprehend “trespassers” in the South China Sea.\n“I am appealing to the Chinese government not to force that on us because it would only escalate the tension.”\n","json":[{"type":"p","children":[{"type":"a","attribs":{"href":"https://www.scmp.com/topics/philippines","title":"","target":"_self","data-entity-uuid":"3a83a0e4-5306-449f-82e7-7e069dc9e7f3","data-entity-type":"taxonomy_term","data-entity-bundle":"topics"},"children":[{"type":"text","data":"The
